Chapter 24.
On the care of the native flesh in the nose.
When the native flesh of the nose has been cut by various instruments, there is something which is absorbed by the scorpion's feet: from this there is a cancerous tumor, a stone, and flesh of a dusky color: from this there is what is soft flesh, not of a dusky color.
Therefore, in that which there is soft flesh, not fraudulent, neither cancerous, you must make the diseased part rest between your hands facing outwards, and open the nose through its opening, and push out the tongue into those flesh: then draw them out to the exterior, and cut away what you have seen with a fine sharp instrument from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed
If anything remains of that from whose excision is impossible: then cut it away with a fine instrument with ease, until nothing remains of it If blood therefore dominates, or if there is a thick clot: then oppose it with something which will stop it: or with bad clots, then burn them with the cautery, until the blood is stopped, and remove all the flesh.
Then throw into the nose after the incision of the flesh, water or liquid. If the nose is opened, and there is humidity coming from it until the palate, then you know that it is healed
If indeed the humidity does not penetrate, according to what is proper, then you know that there is still native flesh in the upper bones of the nose, which has not been removed with the instrument during the incision
Therefore you must introduce a thread of fine caliber which has some thickness to it, and tie a knot in it: and place between every knot the quantity of digit, or less: and cause the diseased part to bleed, by sending one end of the thread into the nose with a hook, or in any other way that is possible for you to do after you have made it similar to a burn: and draw out the foul matter until it comes out to the outside, and go out from under the chin.
Many do like him in schools, just as the young Attomus: and it is easy for him who wishes.
Then place two extremities of the child, one that projects over the nose and the other that remains in the nose And hold the flesh closed with knots that are in the limb. Do this until you know that the flesh is closed with knots in the limb
Then draw out the thread: and put in the nose after draining blood the amount that you drank in the Egyptian unguent: do this three days, or more, until the unguent has eaten away all that remains of the flesh
Then put in the end of the matter in the nose a lead cannula for a few days, until it heals.
If, however, treatment is required that demands it, use it.
For the head wound, when oil and other medicines are distilled in the nose, do this from silver or from lead: and let it be like fine pepper, open like a conch, and similar in size
And if you wish to make a closed cannula, like a tube
The tube of the instrument for making the head wound is full, closed: which has a handle in its rear, with which you grasp it, when you heat it in oil, or when you wish to put some ointment in it, and in the fluxes.
Head wound.
Many are born in the extremity of the Thelulean nose, and grow large, and increase with days, until the wound becomes like its appearance
And therefore it is necessary, that in the commencement of its apparition, it should penetrate all its tissues. Then place above it, or cauterize, or apply a burning medicine which is in its place.
If, however, its incision is delayed until it has increased in size, then consider whether it is stony, hard, dark brown, insensitive: then do not touch it with the iron: for the tumor is cancerous
Many therefore are the waters which issue from the tumor, which is the cause of great malice above that which it has had
And if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark brown, and the waters which issue from its incision are possible throughout all of it, then evacuate the patient, and cut it without fear, without pain: and care for the place which exudes and contracts, until it is healed
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and the lip, and the ear.
You know that when resolution of continuity takes place in some cartilaginous matters, it causes little in that operation, except in some men.
It is necessary therefore, when anything has happened from this, that you consider whether it is a wound which has adhered with its blood: and then join the two edges of the wound with suture: then care for it until it is healed
And if the resolution of continuity of the lips has already been separated, and each part is sound: then it is necessary to strip away every part from its apparent skin, until the blood clots Then join the two lips with suture, and string them, and smear above it the blood of a dragon, and olive oil, finely ground: and place above the ground the edge of a plaster of Paris, and any other solid plaster: and leave it bound for two days, or three Then loosen the thing itself, and change the medicine: and discharge the thing, until the threads are severed by themselves. Then take care of the thing with the unguent, until it is healed.
The way of stitching is that you gather the solution of continuity together, or with an obtuse angle, as we say in the gut suture: or gather the solution together with the thread, as you have been told.

Concerning the reason for teeth decaying with iron.
When they gather together on the surfaces of teeth from both inside and outside, and between the gums, their outer layer becomes rough, dark, and sometimes they decay, and black spots appear, until this corruption reaches the gum and causes the teeth to become loose.</start_of_turn

Chapter XXX.</start_of_turn
Concerning the extraction of wisdom teeth.</start_of_turn>
It is necessary that the dentist remove the tooth from its pain with all his skill, and afterwards to eradicate it. It is not in his power to remedy the pain of the tooth when it is eradicated: for it is a sublime substance. Therefore, when there is no other remedy for the eradication of the tooth, it is necessary to eradicate it, even if the patient dislikes it, in order to establish his health and give him confidence concerning the painful tooth.
Many patients dislike the patient when he eradicates the tooth, thinking that he thereby removes the cause of the pain. But the pain does not vanish until the diseased tooth is eradicated.
We do not wish to be like those who are skilled in phlebotomy, but are ignorant of surgery.
When it is certified to you that the tooth is painful itself, then it is necessary to sacrifice around the tooth with sponges, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is severed from all sides: then move the tooth with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first slowly, until you have crushed it
Then place the large forceps in a good place, and hold the patient's head between your knees: which you have done well, because it will not move. Then extract the tooth according to its right position, so that you do not break it
If it does not come out, then grasp some of these instruments and introduce them under it from all sides with ease, and try to move it, as you did at first.
If, however, the tooth is perforated or corroded: then it is necessary to fill the hole with cloth: and tie it with the end of a fine thread, so that it does not break in the hour of your haste with the forceps.
And it is necessary to be skilled in the art of scarification around the gums from all sides much.
Be strong, and guard your fortitude, lest you be overcome by it, and let some remain of it to return upon the infirm from that which is greater than its first pain.
Do not do that which the unskilled do, the phlebotomists of your faction, in their frenzy, and do not be overbold in its eradication, for we give you that which we have mentioned
Many indeed make them grow worse upon great men's diseases, which are more difficult to cure, and leave none of them, or take away some of them: or take away the whole with some of the teeth of the jaw. Thus have we seen many do
Then it is to be joined after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If blood flows from any place (for many indeed flow from its place), then there is something from the limb which must be taken away with it: and if this does not suffice, burn it if it does not yield the limb.
The tweezers which you must use to grasp the tooth in the first place are long-necked and have short handles, thick, so that they do not bend when pressure is put upon them: they are like robust handles of tongs, until when they are bound with the tooth, they do not bite themselves, nor bend: short-necked, and are made of iron or hammered out of sheet iron, directed towards the extremity: and in their extremities are teeth, some of which enter into some of them, and bind the constriction loosely, moderately strong
And when the extremities become like the beak of a bird called a ciconia, and are strong constrictions.

Chapter 33.</start_of_turn>
On the Retension of Teeth with Wire.
When it happens that the front teeth are fractured by percussion or in any other way, and the patient cannot bite without pain: it is necessary to ligate them with wire. For silver is easily tarnished, and putrefies after a few days: but gold never changes.</start_of_turn>
And let the wire be of medium thickness and of such a size as to grasp between the teeth.</start_of_turn
And the form of the ligation is that you take the wire and pass its double through between two healthy teeth: then you tie it with two extremities of the wire between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you have secured it until you reach the healthy tooth of the other side.</start_of_turn And do it again until you reach the part from which you began: and keep your hand steady with wisdom, so that it does not slip: and make your ligation as close as possible to the root of the teeth, so that it does not move.</start_of_turn
And cut off the excess of the wire with shears: and join each end together and bind them easily: and hide them between the healthy tooth and the moved tooth, so that it does not affect the tongue: then release the tension as much as remains.</start_of_turn
If however it is resolved or fractured, ligate it with another wire and keep it stable in this way for a whole time.</start_of_turn
This is the shape of a tooth, and the figure of the return of two sound teeth and two moving teeth, like water.

When some bone or cartilage has been adjusted: and it is made like the shape of a tooth, and is placed in its place, from which the tooth is supported and attached, as we have said: and it remains, and serves its purpose for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
When this ligament which happens under the tongue is cut, it is either natural, as it is born with man, or accidental, from an injury which has healed badly
And the operation in this case is to open the mouth of the patient in your hand, and lift up his tongue: then cut through that ligament of nerves across the breadth, until the tongue is freed from its retention
If there is something hard and nodular in this, and if that comes from the consolidation of an injury: then pull out that piece of bone, and find that across the breadth, until you cut through the ligament and the knot is freed
Take care that there be no perforation in the depth of the flesh, and that you do not cut an artery there, and that blood flow does not increase
Then wash the wound with rosewater, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then put under the tongue a piece of linen cloth, which will hold the patient throughout the night: so that it does not solidify again
If blood flow increases, then put a plug over the wound. If blood flow increases, then cauterize the wound with a lancet, which is suitable for this purpose. Then care for the wound as usual until it heals.
Chapter 5.
When there occurs under the tongue a small swelling which absorbs the small ranula, hindering the natural action of the tongue: and perchance it may grow so large that it fills the mouth.
The operation for this is to open the mouth wide open: and consider the swelling.
If there is blood of a red or black colour, hard, which does not yield to the touch of the infirm: do not touch it, for it is cancer
If it inclines towards white, in which there is moisture, then throw into it a fine sponge, and press it down with the thumb, and remove it from all sides If in the hour of your operation blood flow increases, then put over it a plug of felt, until the bleeding stops: then return to your operation, until you have removed it with its complement
Then it shall be bound up with vinegar and salt: and care for it with the usual treatment which is suitable for this, until it heals

Chapter 7
When the catarrh descends to the small intestine, and there is an apophysis, and it is long: then it is called a perpendicular one: and if it is in the lower part large and round, then it is called a loop
When it is cured with what we said in the explanation, and the cure does not take hold: and the apophysis is softened from it, and is thin: then it is necessary to make an incision for it
And what is aggregated round about it, and is not of its length, and is bloody, or of a yellowish color, or black, and has no sensation: then it is necessary to make an incision of it For in this there is fear of the sick person
It is necessary therefore when the blood flows according to the mode which I mentioned from the white, and its length, that you make the sick man sit facing the sun: and pull his tongue with an instrument, of which I spoke before: then fix the loop in one hand, and draw the other downwards: and make an incision with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the incision of two friends
And it is necessary that you do not make an incision from that, except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition
If you cut too deeply from this, it causes swelling and speechlessness.
And thereafter, after the incision, use what we have said concerning the two amygdales: and take care of it until it has healed.
If, however, the patient becomes feverish from the incision: then it is necessary to use your skill in cauterization without fear or hesitation
And the manner of cauterization in this is not except with acute water: and it is that you place the head of the patient in your lap. And the extremities of it on which the medicine is placed are like the cochlea of a radius
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the limb itself: and the patient lies upon his side, so that the saliva may run out from his mouth from the medicine itself, and not go down into his gut and harm him
And hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first apply some quantity of an hour over the limb, until you see that it is becoming black, and stop the biting of the medicine.

Do this many times, until you achieve what you desire from the cauterization of the limb. Then discharge the dressing: for it itself will grow and fall away in three days, or four.

The outside should be dressed with stitches, and the inside with gargles, until healed.
This is a safer and more effective treatment than a deep incision and is less dangerous
And sometimes even the wound itself will heal even better with this than with cautery or incision: for it is healed with fumigation in this way. Take calamine, hyssop, origanum, rue, saffron, chamomile, and fennel, and grind them together in a pot, and pour vinegar over them: let them boil: and cover the pot with a lid: and make a hole in the middle of the pot, above which place an instrument that is concave according to this manner
Make it from silver or brass: and introduce the extremity, in which there is a pomegranate, into the bone of the patient, until the vapor reaches the wound above the canula, until the wound is much obscured
Then repeat over it many times, until it heals.
And be careful, do not make this treatment at the beginning of an abscess. For in the abscess let the heat be added: and do not do this except when the abscess has cooled down
If however this instrument is not present to you, then take a needle and make in the extremity of it bark of willow: so that the bone of the patient does not burn: for the bark of willow hinders the vapor from burning the bone: and this is from a good treatment with safety.

On the extraction of thorns and bones from the gullet.

On the Extraction of the Blood-Sucking Worm.

Properly, therefore, and of his own will, and cared for himself, until he was well: and nothing happened to the handmaid except that she was thrown into the water. She returned after a day to her former condition.
Accordingly: there is no fear in the Epiglottis section.

Chapter 52.
Of the care of the great vessel.
The great vessel arises from multiple ulcers. It proceeds either from rents of the liver which are above the belly: or it proceeds from the intestines, according as happens in other ruptures Or from the blood which flows from the vein, or from the artery, according as is prescribed Or from the swelling which is contracted in it
If it is because of a rent of the liver, and the outlet of the bile: then its color is similar to that of the body: and it is soft, without pain: and appears in various places If it is because of the bursting of the intestines, then its site is as we have narrated, with great variety When you thrust your finger into the wound, it is hidden; then it returns, and often makes a sound. And it swells greatly at the entrance of the bath, and causes great suffering.
If however it is because of moisture, it is soft, then it does not hide when you thrust your hand in, neither is it constricted, neither does it swell.
If it is because of blood, then with these signs the clot appears black at the edge of the nail
If however it is because of dead flesh: then it is a large, thick, hard clot, and it forms according to the quantity of one
If however it is because of wind, then its touch is soft.

Of the incision of the super Herniam aquosam

Chapter LXVIII.
Regarding the softening of the skin of the testicles
Many people soften the skin of the testicles because of the meat that is between them, and it makes their appearance better.

Chapter LXIX
Regarding Castration
You know that castration is forbidden in our law: and for this reason I will not mention it in this book: and it is not necessary to mention it, except for two cases.
Of which one is that it is in the science of medicine when it is asked about it: and to know its treatment when it happens
And the other is that we often do not approve of castration for some animals, because of our laws concerning them, such as horses, donkeys, and cats, and similar animals.
I say therefore, that the Castration is accomplished in two ways. Or by excision. Or by cutting, and sectioning.
But in that way which is done by attrition, the operation is: we shall make the animal sit in warm water, until the testicles soften and relax, and hang down: then we shall grasp the testicles with our hands, until they are resolved and do not appear under the touch
In castration by cutting and sectioning, it is necessary to hold the animal firmly, and press the skin of the testicles with the left hand.
Then tie the cord, and dry over every wound a single stitch, until two testicles have descended: then cut through them after you have excised both, and do not leave anything outside except for the fine blood vessels that are above the testicles.
And this way of castration is better than that which is done by attrition: for in attrition there is a risk that something will be left behind from the testicles, which the animal may desire for coitus.
Then care for the wound, until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
There are two methods for the care of Hermaphrodites.

As for the second kind of men from whom urine comes, which is in the testicles, there is no operation, nor any treatment for it at all

Chapter LX.
Concerning the treatment of the embryos of the womb, according to an unnatural form of birth.

Thus I say. When a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from a piece of stone or something similar, and if only the skin is torn: and it is a great wound, and the time is great for an inflamed swelling to increase: then stop, and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately: and do not delay this: and extract from him from the blood according to the quantity of his virtue: if there be nothing to prevent it, especially if there be little blood issuing from the wound. If, however, some of the intestines or all of them have been cut through, and the guts are hanging out: then gather the lip of the skin with the suture over the top of the gut, and do not touch the gut, but rather equalize it, and reduce it to its natural position.</start_of_turn> Then bind it tightly with a bandage, and let it go in a few days.</start_of_turn Then care for it with unguents in which there is stypticity and drying, until it heals.</start_of_turn
If, however, a wound has made an imprint in the bone, then examine it: then if there are fragments of bone in it, extract them all, or whatever can be separated from it: and what cannot be prepared, leave it, until the wound has healed, and the swelling has subsided.</start_of_turn

Of an ulcer in Spatulis.
If there is percussion of the lance or knife, and its blood is deep, then consider it seriously. If blood comes from the ulcer when the patient breathes, then you know that the ulcer is mortal
If it is not deep and blood comes with it: do not at once put powder on it, neither bind it up, lest the blood remain in its depth and be fatal. Put on it a strong unguentum and heal it in two days.</start_of_turn>
If there is no unguentum there, then put in the mouth of the ulcer a little wool, so that it may absorb what comes out of it from the humilities: and make the patient sleep over the ulcer so that it may heal what is gathered in it.</start_of_turn
If after three days, or more, the patient does not have a spasmus or tremor of the cord, nor a bad stricture in his breath, and has all the other symptoms good, then you know that the ulcer is healed.</start_of_turn Treat it with care and with remedies, and with other cures, until it is healed.</start_of_turn>
If in the end its healing is excused, and it always opens, then you know that there is a fistula.</start_of_turn Treat it from its chapter.</start_of_turn
And if the ulcer is from a wound of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then treat it as if it were sutured, if it is large: or with powder if it is small.</start_of_turn
If, however, he has made an imprint in the bone, and it breaks off from it: then inquire about the nail, and prepare to extract that fragment, if it is separated. If it is not separated, then leave it, until the nail softens, so that the extraction may be easier.
The rest of the nails which have become loose in the other limbs, judgment concerning these is in the treatment, is the judgment which we have mentioned.
Concerning the nails of the belly, and the exit of the intestine, and the evacuation of them.
The fracture which happens in the belly is great, medium, or small.
From the great, indeed when the intestine comes out, or the number of intestines.
Therefore comes the introduction of the intestines, and the suture is more difficult and arduous
And the small fracture also becomes difficult in another way Therefore, it follows that the reduction of these is more difficult if you do not promptly restore them to their place in their introduction: for they become swollen, open, and thickened: therefore the entrance of them becomes difficult
Therefore, it is better to fracture the medium than the large or small: for with the medium, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is with the other two kinds
You know that the suture of the belly is according to four modes
Two sutures are common for the suture of the belly, and for the suture of the rest of the limbs: and two other sutures communicate with the rest of the limbs alone Truly are these things proper with the sutures of the belly: and I tell you one also with its explanation and declaration.
When a wound is small and something comes out of the intestine through it, it is difficult to reduce it: then that is because of one of two things. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we have said: or because there is an inflation in the intestine because of the cold of the air.
When it is like this, it is necessary to heat it up, so as to soak the sponge or the cloth wet in warm water only Or boil in water a little barley, some pepper and some spike, and embrace with these the intestine, until the inflation is resolved.
When it makes this drink which has a astringency: and it is better than plain water in resolving the inflation.
When the inflation has been resolved, then moisten the intestine with the water in which it has been boiled, or with oil For in this way it makes the entrance of it with a little thing more gentle.
If however it is excused its return after this cure, then find in the disruption a little thing, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
The first cure is better when it is prepared, than a section: and does not make a forcing to cut after necessity and the defect of the instrument.
Let one part be acute, which is a circle, and the other part not acute, and the extremity soft should not be like that of Spatumilis, but rather be more obtuse like chaff: and that is like the almond, as the drink is like that.</start_of_turn>
When the wound is thus increased, and the intestine is introduced, then it is necessary to reduce it according to its natural form in its own place, if possible above it: for that is better.</start_of_turn>
When the disruption is wide in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the patient lie upon his back, and raise his legs higher than his head. If it is in the upper part of the belly: then place his head high, and his chest higher than his lower parts
Similarly, if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the belly: then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is should be higher than the other part.
It is necessary in large wounds and in medium wounds.
In small wounds, place it according to what is prepared for you
Then make present between your hands a more pious minister who holds the entire disruption with his hand: and join his lips together. Then draw from it that which follows the suture something after something: and this is the common way of stitching one.
And it is necessary to take an awl or the number of awls according to the quantity of the wound's breadth Then let go from the extremity of the disruption the quantity of the little finger, that is, the physician: and fix the awl one beyond what you put into the vein in two extremities of the skin, when two extremities are put through the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate from another part, and you have gathered up two extremities of the skin, and two of the veins: and there are four orders formed
Then string with double thread around the circumference of the awl several times from two parts together, until two lips of the wound are gathered together by a decent aggregation.
Then let go of the length of the finger also, and fix in its place another one: afterwards draw back the thread with the needle, as you did with the first needle. Do not cease to do this until you have completed the constriction of the whole wound
And let there be medium-sized needles between the length and the thickness: because the fine needles easily slip through flesh: and the thick ones are difficult to pass through the skin: therefore it is necessary that there be medium-sized ones in the thickness, and length.
It is not to be permitted that the needle be fixed at two contiguous parts of the skin close to each other: that the flesh should not be pierced too quickly, and that the wound should not be opened before its consolidation, and that it should not be prolonged even with the suture, for fear of hindering the consolidation of the wound
Then cut off the ends of the needles, so that they do not injure the patient during his sleep: and put over it little balls of wool from all around, which will hold the ends of the needles: and let go of them, until you know that the wound is consolidated
And this kind of suture with needles of this sort is more convenient in small wounds For it is sufficient in the suture of these to make use of one needle, or two, or about as many as are needed.
The second suture is more common, that it joins together four extremities: two of skin and two of fat in one wound, with a needle in which the thread is equally distributed in thickness and length.
Then when you have pierced with the needle the four extremities, reduce the needle from the part from which you began, so that the retouched thread may fall from the upper part of the wound: so that the suture be like the suture of cloth which we make when we string up cloth, and put between every suture as much of the length of the smaller finger as is necessary
Men know this suture.
When such a suture has been made, men know that it is sufficient to bind up what has been wounded by the knife, which has caused a wound on the belly, and which has been torn away from its substance by the greater part of the palm of one hand: and that it has escaped from the intestines about two palms from the middle intestine, and that there is a tear in the middle of the belly.
Therefore, I put it outside the wound for twenty-four hours
It was consolidated in the space of about fifteen days: and I cared for it, and it healed: and afterwards it remained sound for many years, being in its proper place.
And the doctors said that it would not heal
And by a miracle, I cured it without ointment Because I was in a place where unguent was not to be found, neither was there any of the physicians there I put upon the wound a coat of wool twice a day, and I bathed it with honey water until it healed
There are two kinds of sutures which I shall tell you about according to the narration of the sermon.
And it is like this: you begin with a sharp suture: and you pass the point from outside to the inside part
When you have thus pierced the skin, and in the wound you have made a loop according to the length and breadth of the whole body: you draw out the point of the needle in the side in which you put it in, and you make the point penetrate from the inside part to the outside in the opposite side.
When you have thus made the point penetrate, then make it penetrate once more in the same place from the outside part to the inside: and draw out the point which is in this side, and make the needle penetrate from the inside part to the outside in the opposite side: and make it penetrate just as you made it penetrate in the point of the needle which is in the side, until it penetrates the whole thing
Then commence from this side also from the same limb, and bring your finger along with the extremity, which is from the heel on the other side, and extract the point from the skin as it has penetrated.
Then reduce the limb to this side also, and bring your finger along with the extremity which is on the other side, with the heel: and extract the point from the skin which has penetrated, and make that part whole again and the other: and make that part whole after the other, until your entire limb is whole according to this example.
And the end of this suture is that you make your sutures like those which are made for the skin: so that you make one from one side of the limb and the other from the other side, until you have completed it.
The second kind of proper suture which Gale has said.
This is how he has said it, that you must make your sutures like some of the physicians do, because they collect every part in proportion to itself, and apply one extremity to the other extremity, and the heel to the other heel. And that is how it is done according to what I have told you.
It is necessary that you make the point of the needle penetrate from the outside inwards in this part, and that you draw out two extremities of the limb. Then withdraw the point and make the needle penetrate from the outside inwards through two extremities of the limb, namely both of them
Then withdraw both of them, and make them penetrate both from the inside outwards through the two other extremities which are on the opposite side
And this is the best way of making a suture, and is the suture which requires remembering
This is indeed the word of Galen.
Some have said this. The suture of the belly is corrected with two squares. That is, insert one thread into each of the two squares: and begin with the insertion of the needle from the side which is towards you, and make it penetrate until it reaches the opposite side, according to the suture which is to be made in the skin equally
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly: then the suture of that is more difficult than that of the rest of the belly
The judgment of the healing of a wound is the judgment of the rest of the wounds.
That is, when you follow the wound with its own blood, before it changes itself: and reduce the intestines, and touch it, and place over it the powder of burnt barley.
If the air has already changed the wound: then place over it some unguents which are present, until they make pus: and the threads fall out, and they are solidified with earth, and the matter is healed. Then care for it in the same way as the care of the rest of the wounds, until it is healed.
But if there are no medicines at hand, then place over it immediately, from which it begins to emit pus, a little bit wet: and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.</start_of_turn>
For you will not find any other medicine in many conditions, when the wounds are simple.</start_of_turn>
If however you are afraid that the wound communicates with the principal parts in the lesion, then you must plunge fine wool yarn into lukewarm oil, or in rose oil: and put it around the circumference of the parts which are between the groin and the flanks.</start_of_turn
If he feels pain, or swelling, or corruption in his intestines (for such things often happen to him), then let him drink a cluster of black vinegar, warm: especially if the corruption has already progressed in the intestine, and an opening has been made through to its cavity.
You know that that which is from the great intestine is easier of healing: and that which is from the small intestine is more difficult of healing.
But the intestine which is called the jejunum, does not recover from a wound which has happened to it throughout: and this is because of the multitude of its veins, and the magnitude of them, and because of the fineness of its body, and nearness to its own nature of nerves

About care for wounds and fistulas.
Thou knowest, that every wound, or abscess when it grows old and becomes hardened, and becomes a wound, and does not consolidate, and emits pus always, and does not burst: then it is called generally in every part of the body a fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.</start_of_turn>
And a fistula is indeed according to truth a filtered nodulation, hard, white: with which there is no pain, which has the nature of the wing of a bird: and for that reason they call some of them Risati, that is, wings.</start_of_turn
And in some hours it is humid, which emits pus.</start_of_turn And perhaps it bursts in some hours.</start_of_turn And when it is this humid matter is much, and is little, and is large, and is soft.</start_of_turn
And I say before you, whenever I have mentioned an ulcer, that no ulcer whatsoever that does not heal by natural means, neither is it born in that flesh, is anything other than through one new cause.
And one of these is because of the scarcity of blood in the body: or because of the wickedness of the blood: or because between itself and over the lips there is tough flesh, hindering the birth of flesh: or because there is much pus and dirt: or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the material that issues from it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is inappropriate for its treatment: or because corruption is falling in the region, which is a kind of pestilence: or by the nature of the region itself, as it happens in Caesaraugusta, in which there is great difficulty in digesting food and the healing of wounds is delayed by nature: or because there is one bone, or many bones.
And I have stated all of these causes and signs and their treatments with the physicians in the discourse: and it is fitting for you to write the treatment of fistula and hernia, which is done by one operation with the hand.
You know therefore that fistula can occur in all the members of the body. For there are fistulas which extend to great veins and arteries, or nerves, or fat: or to the intestine, or the bladder, or to some of the ribs: or to some of the vertebrae, or to some composition of joints: such as the hand and foot. And there are those which arise from the proximity of the principal limb and the like
For such fistulas are according to this manner: they arise from difficult and non-healing ailments, and do not receive treatment, and the care for them is laborious and ignorant
If, however, any fistula does not reach these parts which I have mentioned, and if you hope for its recovery and trust in its treatment, and wish to attempt its healing, then attempt it from the air or from the iron, for the fistula tends in this way. Inquire about it with the physician herself.
If, however, in the Fistula there is tortuositas, then you must investigate it with a slight weight of lead. For the lead, on account of its slightness, will alleviate the harshness of its body and will curve around the tortuositas.
If the Fistula has many orificia, it is not possible to make a significant sign upon it with a slight weight Therefore clench from the opening of the orificia towards the other opening of the orificia.
For the humidity that you clench with will go forth from the other opening of the orificia and will run out from the body.
Then inquire precisely in what way it is possible for you: whether it is an os or a nerve, and whether the Fistula is long or short: or whether it is a single Fistula which has many orificia, and is stretched over all of it, with the sum of your strength: with your inquiry which you make to the patient, and the finding of pain in compression with your hand over the place, and similar signs and symptoms of the accident.
When you have stood in all these positions truly, then you will come to the cure according to your confidence: and it is that you consider then, if the apparent Fistula is close to the juncture, or far from the juncture from a nerve, artery, vein, or any place which I have mentioned to you: then dry the Fistula according to what has been said before in this narration, and take away what is in it from filtration, and corrupted blood, which is born from the tissue in its opening, or the pus, and similar things: and care for it until it is healed
If, however, the Fistula is of great depth and is according to the rule: then it is necessary to cut away the part in depth as much as is possible Then loosen her from all corrupted flesh.
Then administer unguents bound up in acute medicines: and introduce them until they reach the depth of the fistula which you have comprehended with the iron: do this until the acute medicine has eaten away everything that remains in the depth of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore her with unguents which generate healthy flesh, until she is healed.
If she does not heal thereby, then burn her according to what we have narrated
If indeed the cause of the fistula is bone, and you suspect this: then inquire about it, and dry it up as I have told you: if you are not forbidden by anything from the bone, or nerve, or main limb, as we said
If indeed it is found that the bone is in some degree corrupted and black: then burn it until the corruption is entirely gone Then care for her with that which nourishes her, until she is healed.
For if she does not heal, and pus comes out like she used to: then you know that you have not reached the entire eradication of the corruption.
Therefore take care of this secondarily, and make an investigation of the cause of it, and loosen her with all your strength
Then restore her. If indeed she heals, that is good: and if not, then you know that the corruption is in a deep part of the body, which you have not followed. It is not enough for you in this matter, except you dismiss her like the nature of things.
If indeed the small bone is corrupted, and you think it possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps which fit to it
If there are many bones, then you must perform attraction on them all, and do not leave anything out with your skill.
If, however, there is some moisture in them [the bones], and there is no fear of incision, or of error: then you must cut through those moist parts and flesh, if there are any, and free the bones.
If you are not prepared to extract the bones at this hour, then put over them something which will cause putrefaction to it: and leave it until it has putrefied, that is until the soft parts surrounding the bones have rotted away from the bones and the moisture
And keep the cloth so that it does not harden. And be cautious as you make the incision in your operation. If you wish, put over it, if you will, some sulfur which has been ground with oil: or immerse it in Egyptian unguent, or in some other unguents of your choosing
For when these moist parts putrefy, and are separated from the bones, the swelling will subside, and the attraction of the bones will be restored
If, however, there is a large bone, such as the shin bone or the thigh bone, which are corrupted: then take away that part entirely, until the blackness and corruption have vanished And then restore the cloth.
If, however, that which is corrupted is a large part, and the corruption has extended to the marrow of the bone: then it is necessary to cut through it completely, and to cut away all that is corrupted: then care for it until it has healed
And I tell you, what happened to me in my leg: that you may take it as an example and an assistant for your operation.
A youth of about thirty years of age suffered pain in his leg, because something had become loose within his body, that is, from within, until it reached the thigh, and there was discharge of great quantity: there was no external cause.
It has been prolonged thus long as with the mistake of the physicians, until the abscess opened, and many materials came out together with it.</start_of_turn> And after that, his recovery took place, until the thigh was ligated, and many holes were made in it: from which all sorts of pus and moisture were emitted, and the body was moistened.</start_of_turn>
He was treated by many physicians for two years, and no art of hand was found in this matter until I saw him, and I saw that his thigh was black, and that various matters were flowing from his holes in great quantity: even all the hair of his body was falling out, and his complexion was yellow.</start_of_turn>
There was an attempt to reach the cavity of one of his wounds, and the attempt reached the bone.</start_of_turn
Then I examined all his holes: and I found that they were all communicating with each other throughout the thigh.</start_of_turn
I treated him accordingly, and I cauterized over one of those holes, until I reached a part of the bone: and I found that it was corrupted: it was corroded, discoloured, rotten, and perforated, until it had penetrated as far as the marrow.</start_of_turn
I extracted what was detected from the bone, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.</start_of_turn
And I concluded that there was no other blemish in the bone except that corruption, which I excised and cauterized.</start_of_turn> I have eradicated it.
Then I order restoration of the wound with consolidated spaces for a long time with the physicians. It is not consolidated yet.
Then I tried, and found upon the second examination: and I observed continuous corruption with the bone: and I saw that there appeared to me also from that corruption
I am making an attempt to restore it It is not restored yet
I found therefore again on it, and I did not cease to cut the bone piecemeal, and I attempted to restore it: and it is not restored, until I cut from the bone round about the palm of the hand, and I extracted that with the marrow
Then I restored it with the physicians, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and no further operation was necessary concerning this matter, except for the disposition of the weakness of the patient, and his little tolerance, and my fear of death for him: because it was happening to him at all hours from the excess of vomiting of bad evacuations.
He is now healed completely: and was made in the place of the broken bone, and a good disposition was made of the body: and its strength returned: and it rested in its dispositions: and did not cause him any hindrance in walking or any other movement from that time forward

The incision should be made close to the corrupted area, but not on the concave side of the bone, for it should not be visible to the eye and must not impede the cutting of the saw

If the corruption is at the joint, saw through the corrupted joint, and saw the bone where the incision continues on the other side.

Properly therefore with the incision itself, and likewise if the cause of the corruption is from a percussion of some venomous creatures: such as a scorpion of the sea, and vipers, and spiders, and the like.

And likewise if it happens in the palm, then cut off the palm from the root of the bone, and not leave space for the corruption, until it has seized in the bone of the forearm
And if it comes to this, then cut off the forearm at the elbow in its joint
But if the corruption has passed and you see it around the elbow: then in this is the death of the patient. And to manage beyond this from treatment, is the quantity of the poison.
And likewise do the same with the foot, when the corruption comes to the finger, then cut off the finger at one of the nodes: and if it has seized in the heel, then cut off the whole foot And if it reaches the knee, then cut off the thigh at the knee joint
If the corruption has already passed beyond the knee, then there is no remedy in this, except dismissal, and to leave the patient to die.

About the care of bones, and the quality of blistering with physicians.</start_of_turn>
When something breaks in some limb by a cut, and the space of the break is prolonged until collection of pus forms: then it is burst, or pierced, and what is in it is ejected, and what remains in it is like a wound: and the skin which is above it is like a thin membrane which has been stripped off: and it does not go to the end in its corruption, neither does it reach the bone, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it is called a Sinus and not a Fistula, truly, but if its space is prolonged until corruption is impressed in some of the parts of the limb, then it is called a Fistula, or swelling.</start_of_turn
And its care is to cut the whole skin as it is rolled back: and especially when it is stripped off, and made like a membrane: and trust that it does not stick to the place because of its corruption.</start_of_turn
If, however, you hope that the skin will adhere and not leave a lasting result of its corruption: and if there is hardness in the skin from the flesh, then care for it with blistering: and consider it as a Sinus.</start_of_turn
If it is great, and the pus which comes out of it is foetid of odor: then clench it with aegyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and zinc, equal parts being gathered in a vessel: and they are boiled over fire until the medicine turns red. And it comes to a state of thickness like honey.
Then take from it what is needed: and dissolve it with water and honey, and clench it with vinegar: and string its orifice, and give the medicine to it in the quantity of two hours
Then draw it out with pressure: do this for several days, until the sinus is healed and the stench is gone.
And when it is clenchized with vinegar and water, when it is not present this medicine: then take ashes of wood or the ashes of ilic tree: and sprinkle them over water. Then let the patient drink, and clench it with these things until you are confident that the sinus is broken
If the patient is not tolerant of the aegyptian medicine, neither of vinegar and water, then clench it with water and honey, to which something of ground zinc has been added Or clench it with honey and wine mixed together, because of the property of honey to heal and cleanse: and the wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is excess of astringency and dryness in it.
When you have done this often, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has passed away: then clench it with that which makes meat appear in it.
And it is fitting to take from gypsum plaster of Daphne: and dissolve it with rose oil and wine vinegar: or clench it with some medicine, and smear over it other unguents, whose properties are written in the treatise on unguents.
If the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that a cannula of the clister cannot enter: then widen it with a small iron; or place within it a piece of lint soaked in Egyptian unguent, or in basilicon unguent, until it is widened.
And in like manner if it is a large opening, then gather its lips together with sutures; and let go from it the quantity which enters the clister without adding more.

He who is above the opening of the sinus must move in every hour, so that it may go out what is gathered in it from the pus: and know whether that which runs out of it is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And thus consider again the position of the sinus itself, whether the master of the limb feels pain there, or whether there is no pain there, and whether there is swelling there, or whether there is no swelling there.
When you thus advance your treatment in this way, and wash away the matter which comes out of the opening of the sinus, then you will know whether the sinus is adhered, or approaches adherence: then you must remove it in its entirety, or within three days: and when you have removed the ulcer, then change the dressing, and renew the ointment.
If, however, something exits from the sinus after a long space of time with a subtle flow, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing approaches, because there are people from whom meat is tardily generated in their ulcers, and there are those who act contrariwise to this.
If, however, something exits from the sinus after many days with undigested pus, then you know that the sinus is not adhered
If thus the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its condition is prolonged: then add in the execution of your treatment, truly let the physicians do what they must, that there be moisture in their medications and dryness in their application: like aloe, when added in excess gives an additional moisture.

Of the treatment of panaritis and nails drawn out, and incision of the added digit, and section.
Panaritis is a swelling which arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps it arises in the other fingers.
When it is prolonged and neglected, it becomes inflamed and produces pus: until it has rotted the root of the nail: and perhaps it has rotted the whole, and perhaps the corruption has extended to the bone, whereupon there is a foul odor: and the nail becomes broad and the color of it becomes dark
When you have treated it as we have said above, and the cure has not taken hold: then it is necessary to cut away with the knife the whole remaining part of the nail Then cauterize the wound afterwards. The cautery is very useful indeed.
If indeed the bone is sound, and the nail is also sound, and the excess of nails impinges on the meat inwards, and they begin to pierce the very flesh, and cause pain: then it is necessary that you place a small radium under the nail which is piercing the flesh, and push upwards: and cut away the flesh with ease, and place over that which remains of the flesh from the burning and corroding doctors, until it is all removed. Then care for it with the nails, until it is healed.
If however corruption has occurred in the bone, then it is necessary that you cut away that bone and extract it: for it will never heal as long as it remains corrupted in that bone

On the Incision of Varices.
The veins involved are swollen, large, and filled with melancholy humors which occur in various parts of the body: and most frequently in the thighs, especially in the thighs of laborers and those who are on the move, and in the limbs which bear weight
It is therefore necessary to administer an excision of the body with black bile somewhat mixed with strong evacuation
Then let there be a bloodletting of the patient with ease of basil.
The cure for these therefore is with iron, in two ways One of them is to cut them open, and draw out the black blood. The other is to elevate the vein, and draw out all of it
The incision itself is done in this way. The thigh is grasped in the first place with warm water, until the thick blood has dissolved Then the limb of the patient is bound from the upper part of the hip down to the lower part of the knee with the varicose vein between. Then the vein is cut through in one place, or two, or three wide sections
And extract the blood upwards from the lower part of the thigh to the upper part, and from the upper part let there be bloodletting of the patient at first Then dry the wound downwards until the amount of blood which has escaped is equal to that which is needed, and as the patient can tolerate the pain.
Then bind up the limb: and command him to abstain from foods which generate black bile: and repeat the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and this will be helpful to the patient
The elevation of that is done in this manner. If the limb is swollen with many hairs, elevate it above the level of the heart. Then introduce it into a bath: or embolize it in the limb with warm water, until it turns red, and expel the veins: or exercise a strong exercise, if there is no bath present, until the limb becomes warm
Then dry the skin along the section of the vein: or at its end near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and remove the vein from all sides, until sensation returns: and it is at the return of sensation that the vein has turned from red to black When it has gone out of the skin, tie up the vein like a cord
Then introduce radium under it, until it rises up and comes out of the skin: suspend it with a piece of gauze, fine
Then dry the other section nearby by three fingers. Then remove the skin from the upper part of the vein, until sensation returns Then elevate the limb with the radium as you did before, and suspend it with another piece of gauze, as you did before
Then dry the other section, or several sections, if you wish Then elevate, and cut the last section near the heel
Then draw out the limb to the section which is above it
And do this until you have drawn out the limb from the superior section, or from all the higher sections, until when it comes out completely: then cut the limb
If, however, it does not obey you to extract it, and you must elevate it: then introduce an awl with a strong double wire, and bind it, and extract it. Or introduce it under that radium and withdraw your hand to every part until it emerges. Take heed, lest you should perforate it.</start_of_turn> For if it is perforated, it becomes difficult for you to elevate it afterwards, and it will enter through that wound again.</start_of_turn>

About the incision above the worms generated under the skin, and it is called the bovine disease.
This disease is called in our land the bovine disease: because it often happens to cows, and is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh.

And about the section over the navel of Nakir.
This ailment is called in our land Nakir: and it is a pain that happens in some limbs: then it changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have seen it, as I have told you. I have been called to a sickly woman in some place. She has discovered her arm swollen, and fluid is gathered in the pit of the elbow bone.
After an hour had passed, there was an increase in swelling, like that of a worm, rising up to the shoulder, more quickly than it is possible for it to run: so that it became like a living silver, when it runs from place to place
The pain therefore vanished from that place, and it was fixed in the shoulder
Then after an hour it ran throughout the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she reported to me that it returned throughout her whole body, as she had seen previously
I marvel at the speed of its change from limb to limb And I myself had never seen such an ailment with the eyes like this in the same way But I have heard many people speak of pain that changes from limb to limb: and I have never seen it with my eyes, as I have seen it in this woman
And I do not believe it is possible, except that it is because of the swelling of the veins of the limbs which are in this case detected from the inside outwards: from which it appears that the sensitivity of the limbs is altered
It is necessary that it does not appear in delicate people and in those who have moist bodies, because of the hidden veins.
When you see that the patient has sensation of pain: then if it appears to you like this which we have said, then bind over it above and below more quickly: and dry above it until the swelling is gone: and cauterize the place.
If you do not aid the eye itself with your eyes: then care for it with the excuse of the body, and with that which ejects wind from the intestines, and carminates it with foul pills and wormwood pills and the like.

Sagittae are diversified according to their kinds and according to the place from which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is such: for some have large teeth and few, and some are concave and some are convex And there are those which have three angles And those which are tongues And those which are stars
The latter are those which are according to the parts from which they fall, is according to two modes. Or rather the parts are from concaved main parts, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and veins, and things like these When therefore it falls into some one of these members, and you perceive the signs of death, which I shall narrate to you afterwards, then it is necessary that you draw out that arrow from the body, for death follows its wound in various positions And when you do not perceive these bad signs, and the arrow is not hidden deeply in the member, then draw it out and care for the wound.
When the arrow has fallen into the brain and has pierced the bone, and has emerged upon the brain itself, it is because its impact has caused vomiting, excitement, buzzing, redness of the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and cholera. And I tell you that I have seen many of these arrows: so that you may know concerning their treatment.

And I tell you what is the quality of the extraction of some arrows: so that you may know the rule and the signification of that which I have not said
For the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with the narration: neither does this book comprehend them, nor does a prudent artificer compare them with many, but rather with few; and with that which is present, rather than that which is absent: and he finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descent of things extraneous to this art when they arise.
I say therefore, that arrows do not go out of the members from which they are fixed, except by two ways. Except by attraction from the place from which they entered. Or by turning the other way around
And whatever comes out from where it entered, that is an arrow proceeding in the flesh: it is attracted and comes out.
If it does not obey the exit at the time when it fell: then it is necessary that you draw it out a few days, until the flesh surrounding it has putrefied: and the attraction of it and its going out will be alleviated
And in the same way if it is embedded in a bone, and does not obey you at the exit, then draw it out also a few days: and do it again every day with the attraction and the movement, until it comes out
If it does not obey you at the exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate all around the arrow in the bone from all sides with a fine awl, until the arrow is loosened Then draw out the [foreign object], and extract it.
If however the arrow has gone away from the body, and is hidden and covered from the sense: then inquire about it with the hand. If however you feel it, then draw out the arrow with some instruments which are suitable for its drawing out.
If however you are not able above the arrow, because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow in depth: and there is no bone, neither nerve, neither vein: then find above the arrow, until the wound be enlarged, and it is possible to draw out the arrow.
And if there are to it ears which hold the flesh fixed in the bone from all parts, from all ingenuity which you can think: and you conceive to draw it out above them, if you cannot draw out the flesh from the fracture of the ears, and the twisting of the ears, until it becomes soft
And you, when you administer the extraction of the Sagitta [arrow], in whatever place it is, then use a twist of your hand with the forceps to the whole parts, until you make it escape: and under the last subtle tension, that the Sagitta does not break, and that it becomes difficult for you to draw it out.
If you are unable to complete the extraction in one hour, then defer it for a few days, until the surrounding tissues have decayed. Of the usefulness of phlebotomy, and of the precautions to be observed in its performance. The quality of phlebotomy, and the accidents which may arise from it, demand that you reflect upon them
Firstly, know that phlebotomy is not to be used except in the preservation of health, and for the treatment of diseases, and for the prevention of ailments.
And that it should be performed in the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the basilic, and the cubital. And thus shall phlebotomy be performed at the beginning of spring upon the feast of Pentecost, in summer and autumn, and in winter after a meal, when signs of repletion appear.
And thus shall phlebotomy be performed on the first or third day, after three hours have passed
Phlebotomy, however, which is administered in fevers, has no fixed hour: but when convenience and necessity demand it, in the night or day, and at all hours, at all times.
And it is not proper to perform phlebotomy on children until they are over fourteen years of age. Neither is phlebotomy to be performed on a pregnant woman except from the fourth month until the seventh month. Neither is phlebotomy to be performed on the aged who are already past sixty years of age
When, however, anyone wishes to undergo phlebotomy for whatever reason, it is necessary to administer a cathartic prior to phlebotomy with a gentle enema, if there is much retained stool: that the intestines do not excrete foul matter onto the principal limbs during phlebotomy.
Neither shall phlebotomy be performed on a patient who is suffering from acute nausea, nor on a drunkard, nor on one suffering from a hangover, until these symptoms have passed from the body Neither on a patient with dropsy
And let phlebotomy be omitted after a fit of colic, vomiting, or flux of the bowels, and after exercise, sexual intercourse, and journeys, and overeating, and fasting, and all that dissolves virtue from the body or from the animal itself
Consider then the viscosity of the humors before this, if the blood is thick with food and drink, and the physicians: if it is possible for it.
Consider then the bath, if it does not forbid it: or some exercise is performed, to refine the blood.
And perform a phlebotomy of it in the chest, around the third of the day, as we have said: and strive that it be evacuated from the chest on that day from all accidental things harmful: such as distress, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily things, such as labor, and pain, and excess, and like things.
And let its scent be present with things that customarily accompany men from aromaticness, and fragrance, and delight and like things to that extent which is possible

Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we said in the prologue of this chapter, to recall the main points and chapters which are necessary for a skilled artisan to arrive at his station concerning the truth of things: for example, those who are proud and arrogant, or who are puffed up with pride, will learn nothing from you, and others will be put off by your arrogance.
When any fracture, dislocation, twist, or falling has happened to anyone: then it is necessary immediately to attend to drawing out the blood and healing the wound itself, or both together: if nothing hinders it, such as weakness of spirit: or because of some other cause, such as a young man who is proud or an old man who is decrepit: or because of the passing of time, or the approach of cold or heat.
Then let him be content with his food of bread, cold herbs, and birds' meat, and with cold drinks: and let him abstain from wine, and from fat meats, and let him drink from food and from every kind of food which fills the veins. Until then, when he is secure from the hot bath, and no longer has any discharge from the wound: then let the sick person return to his ordinary regimen which he is accustomed to.
When the fractured bone begins to heal, then it is necessary for the sick person to eat nourishing and sustaining foods in great quantity: such as porridge made from wheat cooked with water, barley, head cheese, feet, beans, oil, and fish which are fresh: and strong drink, and things like these. With this regimen the coagulation of the fracture will be faster and better.
And you know that bones which are fractured when they are in the limbs of those who are in their prime of life and decrepit, are not able to continue and consolidate according to their natural course because of the wasting away of the limbs: and they consolidate and continue only as much as the hardness of the sinews which remain.
Truth be told, nature itself adds something akin to glue to a fractured bone from every side, which is its toughness, which adheres and contracts it until it solidifies into one piece, and which is bound into one piece until it reaches the limit of strength and firmness, just as it did from the beginning, until it hinders nothing from its operations. For this reason, it is necessary to provide the sick person with food which contains abundance, viscosity and toughness, as we have said.
And know this, that the kinds of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs: for instance, a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head: and a fracture of the chest is different from a fracture of the back: and similarly all the other limbs are diverse one from another. And I come with the recollection of the kind of fracture which is exposed in the chapter which is distinguished from others. The kinds of fractures of bones are also diverse in themselves.
For instance, a fracture may be bowed, besides the fact that they happen in bones with fissures. And sometimes a fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and there are fractures of the wrist, that is, fissures, and protrusions, such as in the pen, when the knife is applied to write. Neither is it from breakage: and it is a fracture with wound and disruption at the head: and it is a slight fracture.
And every kind has its own wisdom, or its own instrument for restoration, according to what is remembered of it, distinguished in its places
From which it is known that the fracture of the bone is its crookedness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it.
When there is no obvious displacement of the bone, no sound from the touch of the hand when touching the bone with movement, and no great pain is found: then there is no fracture, but it is possible that there is displacement or a small fissure. It is not necessary to move the limb with the hand and compression in every case, but rather to place above it something from the physicians which they remember, after which the limb will be bound tightly.
You also know that when a limb is bent and breaks into two, except when the break happens at the same time as the fracture, all the parts are actually displaced from their place. Therefore, it is necessary for you to start from the moment you suspect such a fracture to restore its position, making it even before the swelling has gone down
But if there happens to be a swelling after the fracture, then leave it for a few days until the swelling has subsided. Then restore it as well as you can with whatever gentleness and skill you can manage.
You also know that the restoration of the limb and its straightening is easier than the bone itself, in which the break happened at the same time as the fracture.
Then bind it tightly as you remember it
If there are fragments of bone in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this fractured limb from two parts, if the hand exists, or the foot: or with the hand, if the limb is small: or with two strings, or with a bandage and a hand.
And let your position be such as you place the limb over the place in equilibrium, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the fractured limb are extended: then try to make the fragments rejoin by any means, and in any way you can manage in those places: and be careful according to your strength, so as not to cause greater pain or damage to the patient through your treatment, and try with all your strength to apply one bone to its companion, according to the better forms
It is necessary in this hour that you try and grasp your hand. If there is anything different there, make it right and equal it out according to your strength
Cause the extension to be firm and the compression to be strong, like many stones Many of them make the limb swell for the operation or cause contraction in the limb: as we have seen with these operations many times.
Then bind after the equalization, and lower the operation, and tighten the limb, and rest, and tranquility: and watch over the patient above all Make him lie down, that he does not move in the hour of sleep, and that he rests during the revolution of the stars, and the agitation of the stars, and during the egestion of the stars, and all his movements until his last strength is spent And that the position of the limb should not be the position that follows pain. That is, because when he feels pain in the position of the limb, or an injury: then it is necessary to change it to another position from which he does not feel pain
And that the position of his body should be equal and straight: so that there is no deformation of the limb when restoring it.
The quality of the tightening of the limb is according to what is narrated.
You know that broken limbs are diverse in their parts, and their magnitude, and their shape
Which are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the bone, and things of that kind, then it is necessary to use soft packing, fine Whatever is large within the body, such as the hip and thigh, must have a wide and firm covering.
Since a wide ligament connects the great limb and stretches it evenly from all sides, it does not permit any protrusion of its substance.
Then, after you have evacuated the bowels, apply an ointment that is appropriate for the fracture in a soft cushion over the place of the fracture itself. Then, cover the place of the fracture with three or four layers of ligament, and bind your hand firmly with the ligament.
Then, keep it close to the upper part of the fracture site, and bind less tightly than the tension you used to put on the fractured place. Then, support the limb with a wide bandage from the fracture site downwards, and gradually soften the tension until you can remove the great part of the limb from the wound
Then, do the same thing again, and cover the place of the fracture once more with layers of ligament Then, support the limb from the fracture downwards. And let your operation be in the tension of the bandage and the softening of it, as we said in the first bandage
Then, put between the layers of the bandage a soft cushion, or cloth, so that the fracture is even in it, if there is a curve in it: and if there is no curve in it, do not put anything in it.
Then, cover the previous bandage once more. Then, keep the upper part of both layers of bandage perfectly centered, if there is no swelling, and no hard swelling
If there is swelling or an abscess, then place above it what sits upon that abscess, and it will remove the swelling: and discharge it in a few days. Then string above it those things which you have placed.
And let the things which you have placed be of the middle of broad cane, rounded, formed with wisdom: or let them be of wood whence cribra are made, which are made of twig, or chaff, that is, palm branches, or nails: and let the breadth of every thing be according to this form, and this same figure.
But it is necessary that the thing which is placed above that which is drawn, be greater and broader than the rest of the things: and the length of the thing be according to what is fitting for the limb from its magnitude and thinness
Then string above the things as before, according to the first measure. Then bind above with cords which are slightly slack, according to what we said concerning the measure: and it is that the measure should be greater over the place of the fracture: and the more it is prolonged from the fracture, the smaller the measure
And it is necessary that the cords be of middle thickness and softness, and be of soft wood
For if the cords are thick, like oil from the operation of tying knots in the cord, it is a great error: for the measure falls from the thing which is being measured.
And thin cords do not suffice: for you do not follow with the measure with that which you wish.
And it is not necessary that there should be less than a finger between the things and the thing which is being measured

The common method of plastering is useful for restoring a fracture and is suitable for various temperaments, especially women and children: because it does not cause heat or cold to adhere to it
Take fine flour from the mill, and the bran that hangs on the sides of the millstones during the grinding: mix these in the way that the coarse parts are not too thick, nor too fine. Use this for making the plaster.
