On the treatment of an accident in the head.
There are various kinds of fractures, and their figures are many: and their causes are diverse.
For a fracture is what is made by a blow with a weapon: and the blow raises the whole limb up until it reaches the cartilage, which is under the bone, as does the axis in wood: and for this reason this kind of fracture is called Axial
If an edge of a bone has fractured and has split its face, and the incision has not pierced to its utmost depth: this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, complete fracture.
There are two kinds of fractures, either great or small
A fracture is either a break or a crack: the cause of which is impact with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or something similar.
The latter fracture is either penetrating or non-penetrating: the former when it approaches the nerve which is under the bone, or the latter when it is in the face of the bone. This kind of fracture is also called Kalaan, that is, complete fracture.
And there is also a hidden fracture in the bone under the cover of hair: this is a small fracture, and for this reason it is called the capillary fracture.
And there is also a fracture which is caused by a blow or by percussion of a stone, or by something similar, which breaks through the layers of the bone inwards, and makes a concavity: just as happens with the winds of the air when there is percussion, and some of them go inwards
And this usually happens in the heads of soft bones, such as those of children
And all these kinds of fractures are separated by swelling, and I will remind you of the whole thing in its place.
We recognize all these kinds of fractures by their appearance above the surface, and by their inquiry with tension, and by the excision of the corrupted flesh which is above them
The capillary fracture is recognized by the fact that it is seen on the bone, and is stripped off, and is covered with ointment: the fracture appears black
In treating fractures, consider first the patient's condition: then, if you find signs of obvious significance above the fear of death, such as vomiting of bile, extension, loss of consciousness, cutting of the tongue, syncope, acute fever, discharge of urine, redness of the face, and similar signs in various positions, it is unlikely that they will recover: and if you find no serious signs, even if you hope for recovery, then begin to treat them.
If a patient comes to you wounded in the first instance with a wound: and it is in the cold season, then it is necessary to study the removal of bone before the fourteenth day in every position.
And if it is in the warm season, then it is necessary to take care to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it becomes corrupted due to the marrow, and these are the accidents which we have mentioned
If a fracture of a bone has gone to the marrow touching the brain, and is with small pieces, and attraction: then it is necessary to cut off the broken and protruding part, and draw it out, as we have said
It is necessary to rotate the head of the wounded person: and uncover the bone as much as possible in any way you can: and according to the figure of the wound, and what is best for the patient.
If blood flows from the fracture of the bone, or if there is a swelling of the bone: then oppose it with that which is necessary And it is necessary to apply a place with soaked linen in wine, and wet plaster: and make the wound quiet, until the swelling subsides, and be sure to prevent bleeding. Then begin the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And thus it is done according to one of two methods.
And thus it is done according to one of two methods. The first method is to cut the bone with the Incisor tooth firmly grasping the precipice: this is its form.</start_of_turn>
And thus it is done according to one of two methods. The first method is to hold the Incisor firmly with one hand and grasp the wider part of the Incisor with the other hand: this is its form.</start_of_turn>
And thus it is done according to one of two methods. The first method is to hold the Incisor firmly with one hand and grasp the wider part of the Incisor with the other hand.
And the second method is to have several different Incisors: some of which are wider than others, and some of which are narrower than others: and they should be in the end of greatest sharpness, and should be of iron or steel, and should be used with ease in striking upon the Incisor so that it does not fracture the head and does not damage it.</start_of_turn
If, however, the bone is strong and hard: then it is necessary to perforate its circumference before administering the Incisors, with needles which are called Non-penetrating Needles: and they are not called thus except when they do not penetrate to the end of the bony tissue towards that which is behind it: because the Needle is a rounded extremity above that which is under the acute angle, like the margin, and a small circle prevents it from submerging and penetrating the thickness of the bone.</start_of_turn>
And it is fitting to take from these needles a great number, of which each one is suitable for the quantity of thickness of the bone, until present there be a Needle throughout the whole circumference: the quantity of the acute extremity of which is in length and breadth is according to the quantity of thickness of that circumference.</start_of_turn
And this is the form of the three kinds of needles, or Trepanas.</start_of_turn

And be careful according to your skill, that the wound be soft in your care: neither greasy, nor biting. Of the restoration of the ankle
Chapter 11
Rarely are the bones of the ankle fractured If they are fractured, then the fracture of them is not, except for a comminution at the extremities of them: and they are split in length, and are bent inwards: and there is pain in the place, and swelling: and the leg that is fractured is stiff

If the fracture is only at the extremities of it, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from an equal, until its figure is assimilated to its natural form
If the fracture is along the length, or if it is already bending inwards: then make the patient lie upon his belly, until preparation for the restoration of the fracture is complete. When the fracture is equalized, place upon it a dressing: then place upon it a bandage of wood, or of skin: and tighten the bandage more tightly than you fear a displacement of the fracture, neither a removal of the bandage
And equalize the curvature of them with what supports it, until the bandage grasps them according to the equality: and command the patient to rest upon his back, or upon his healthy side.
If, however, a thick swelling should arise: then stop the extension of it, and of its restoration, until the thick swelling has subsided: and place upon it what has subsided, according as before, then return to the restoration of it: and tighten it as needed.
If indeed it occurs in a bone that is sitting, or is broken, extract it.

On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.</start_of_turn>
The arm is composed of two bones, which are named the ulna and the radius.</start_of_turn

When therefore the upper bone of the forearm is fractured, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is more easy.</start_of_turn

And it is worse when two bones are fractured together.

If the bandage is now softened, and the bone is now removed, and things are like these: then rectify all this with your skill, and consider again.
For if the nourishment is hindered from reaching the limb, because of the excess of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it somewhat, and remove it for a few days, until the nourishment reaches it again, and then bind it again
If, however, no relief has occurred to the sick person anything of what we have said, then it is not necessary to solve the matter, except after twenty days, or thereabouts.
The treatment of the sick person is suspended until his condition improves or after twenty days, or thereabouts. His condition should be allowed to stabilize, and he should be protected from disturbing factors. Let him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture will be healed in thirty days, or perhaps in thirty-two days; and possibly in eighty days All this is in accordance with the laws of complexion and the laws of virtue.
Section on the Restoration of Fractures of the Hand
Know that fractures of the bones of the hand and the joints of the fingers are rare.</start_of_turn> They only occur with great pressure.</start_of_turn
When such a fracture occurs, or a violent pressure: then it is necessary that the sick person sits upright: and let his seat be at an equal distance from him.</start_of_turn Then place his hand on his extended palm.</start_of_turn
Then extend the fractured bones, and let the doctor keep them even until they are reunited in a good union.</start_of_turn
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, if the swelling does not subside.</start_of_turn Then put a support over the place according to the quantity of the place: and cover the hand with a bandage.</start_of_turn
If the fracture is towards the lower part of the limb, round off the edge of the cloth: and command the patient to keep the fractured part pressed against the limb itself: and then bind it with a long cloth.
And let there be a pad of leather, soft as the hand, which shall be applied to the skin with the limb: and bind the limb firmly, as seems necessary
If the fracture is outwards, then it is necessary to place a pad above and a pad below the limb: and keep the hand open and straight. Then put on a bandage, as it is turned over: and extend between the fingers with the ligament.
If the fracture happen to any of the joints of the fingers, if it is the thumb, make it equal as seems necessary.</start_of_turn> And bind it with the limb: and if you see that it is swelling, put upon it a straight pad, thick: so as to elevate the fracture and that it does not move.
If the fracture is of the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or the ring and little finger, make it equal, and bind it with the healthy finger which follows it: or bind them all according to their continuation: and that is better. Or put upon it a thick pad above all these things as we said concerning the thumb.</start_of_turn>
Consider in restoring him and after the position of the aftercare: and oppose with it what is necessary, when anything happens from this, according to the return of its account.</start_of_turn>
Of the restoration of the fracture of the thigh.</start_of_turn>
The bone of the hip is fractured in many places, and is declared to the sense: because it turns towards the inner and outer parts.
Its restoration is done in this way, because it is bound by a ligament above the fracture and a ligament below the fracture. And the limb is restored to its place over its face
Then stretch out all the ligaments that serve at its part according to equality: and this when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If, however, the fracture is close to the root of the hip, then is the ligament towards the hip so that the extension goes up: and the other ligament below the fracture
And thus in the same way if the fracture is close to the knee, then is the ligament close to the knee so that the extension goes downwards
Then the doctor must unite the two bones with his hands, until he has reduced it according to the likeness of its natural figure: and the bone is joined together with a good suture.
Then it is necessary to put on a plaster and a bandage, if it has not already happened to the limb after the fracture If, however, this has happened in that case: then leave it for a few days, until the swelling has subsided, then return to your treatment.
The stricture of it must be such. It must be bound over the fracture with strong material, that is, leather: and two or three times, and remain from that surplus Then bind the thigh, until the heel reaches the root of the knee
And introduce a long thread between the hip and the thigh near the knee downwards, and bring the extremities of the thread from the upper part of both parts. Then rotate over the Knee and Hip which remain from the nourishment.
Then place over the Hip in the place of the fracture its own staples, and place another staple upon the Leg Bone.
Then interlace between Knee and Hip with soft tissues and linens, so as to equal the tension.
Then begin with a strong bind from the middle over the place of the fracture three or four inversions And as much as you lengthen with the ligament from the place of the fracture, let your bind be smaller and softer, and gentler.
Then go to two extremities of the thread which you introduced between the Hip and Knee Tie thus with both three staples which are above. Then stretch with both ends of the thread to the bottom, until you reach the heel of the foot.<start-of-turn> And when you have bound both ends of the staples from the other side: so that the ligament does not be moved from its place.
Then release the bind over it, while it remains, so that it does not cause the limb to break, nor swelling, nor inflation, and the like things.<start-of-turn> For if anything happens from this. Thus do with the solution of it, and correct that which happened from it, as we have said many times before.<start-of_turn>
If indeed on the Bone there is a fragment which projects: then it is necessary, if possible, to remove that.<start-of-turn> And if not, find over that and remove that: and care for that with which it was preceded, until it is healed.<start-of-turn>
When the fracture of the thigh bone is strained, in addition to the stitches which are added, as we mentioned in the aid and arm.
The restoration of the fracture must be such that it does not cause the patient to limp. If only one limb is restored, in addition to the other limb which is applied, then it is necessary that the lame limb should limp always.
You know that the thigh bone is strained in fifty days, or somewhat more, or somewhat less: all this is according to the variations of complexion and the remaining conditions.
Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the knee bone.
You know that the knee bone rarely occurs fractured, but often suffers from multiple abrasions.
If indeed it occurs fractured, then neither a suture nor a split, nor a hole in its parts is to be made This is with a wound and without a wound And the limb should be kept above all in this way
The restoration of it is thus that the disrupted parts be joined together with the digits, until they are united and connected as well as possible with equality and ease, and fabrication
Then place a dressing of flour of barley, or wheat, with albumin or oil: and put on it a round bandage if necessary: and bind it up from above with a suitable strap
Consider all the conditions which we have narrated in the other fractures: thus as the swelling of the limb, and similar things: so that you may counteract all accidents with what is appropriate for it until it is healed
On the restoration of a fractured shinbone.
You know that the shinbone is composed of two bones, of which one is thick and is called the Cruris, and the other is thin and is called the Focile.
And it happens in both kinds of fracture of these bones as it happens in the fracture of both bones of the forearm: and because of this, its restoration is equal, and the operation is one
If both bones are fractured together, the Cruris turns towards all parts
If the thinner bone is fractured, the Cruris turns towards the anterior part
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you will find it below: and it is from this that the parts are not covered by the skin.
Then perform extension, and equalization, and ligation of the ligaments equally.
But you must know that if the fracture of the Cruris is a compound fracture, having many splinters: the extension must be less violent, and more gentle to your alleviation
And in the shinbone there is something more concerning than in the forearm: and it is that when you equal the bones, and have completed all your operation, then take two strips of pine wood which are administered in the making of sandals which are placed under boards: and they are made from palm leaves: and choose those which are of small size, and neither of great size: and let their length be according to the length of the shinbone from the knee to the ankle
And then encase over one-half and two-thirds of the wound with two layers according to its length And put one part of the shinbone from one side, and the other from the other side: and they are from the knee down to the ankle
Then bind two layers of tissue in three places from two extremities and the middle. When the tissues are tightly bound, the knee bends to the right and left, and rests comfortably at a good station.
And thus an anchor is also given from wood according to the length of the leg, and is placed in the knee, to keep it still
And most of all, it is necessary that you do this in a fracture when it is its own bone that is broken
Then consider the knee every day, and study it with great care from its swelling, or from its pain, or from any other sign If anything of this happens to it, then oppose it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills it.
Of fractures of the bones of the foot and the fingers.
The heel never fractures completely
The bones of the foot sometimes fracture when the fracture is complete, and the fingers also sometimes fracture: and it does not happen except by attrition in various positions.
If the bones of the foot fracture, and you wish to elevate them above the level of the body, then put the patient's foot on the prepared earth, like a walker
Then stand still, and place your foot on that which is to be elevated from these bones: then press down, or put on a shoe: and keep it even, until it returns to its place
Then put on a dressing, and put on a cushion above: and place under the foot a board with two flat heads
Then string the thread with the foot firmly bound, after you have plunged the needle with the thread through the tissues, and the rest as needed.
When three or four days have passed, then dissolve the ligament.</start_of_turn> For indeed you will find the bones equal, whether they are fractured or dislocated.</start_of_turn The operation is like an enema.</start_of_turn
If any fingers are fractured, then restore them, and make them equal as before.</start_of_turn Then put your finger on the splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be wider than before: then put under the foot this board as I have told you, and string the thread firmly.</start_of_turn>
If indeed two, or three, or more bones are fractured, then put on each finger a splint of its own quantity, bound with cloth: and string the board at the lower part of the foot: and let it rest on one head of the board outside the foot, in order to keep the retention good.</start_of_turn
And it is fitting that you should remember that I have told you concerning all the things which relate to the fractures which arise from accidents which I have mentioned: and oppose every accident with what is appropriate.</start_of_turn>
Regarding the fracture of the forearm of a woman, and the bone of the elbow, and the swelling of a man.</start_of_turn
When the forearm of a woman is fractured, then make her sit in a square position.</start_of_turn Then incline the thread towards the part of the back of the same side, and keep it back.</start_of_turn
Then let the obstetrix apply pressure to the vulva with his thumb, until he feels resistance: and let him work in the vulva as if with a sphere.
Then let the woman contract and raise her back gradually. This is how the lip will be extruded, until it reaches the vulva like a sphere, and the fracture returns to its place
Then let him put a cushion over her back When he wishes to mix, he should move the lip with ease, then mix: and reduce it as before, in the manner in which you introduced it at the beginning; then return to his cushion first
Do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone will be restored by itself
And if you wish, take a sponge or something similar, and string it over the opening of the wound, and introduce the sponge entirely into the vulva Then blow air through the tube with force, until the sponge is inflated inside the vulva, and the fracture returns to its place
Then let him do the same with the thumb, and let him rest for a few days, as we have said, until he is healed
And when the fracture of the Pectinis bone of a man or woman has happened, then manage in restoring it, and in this way that we have said concerning the bones of the ankle.
And do not conceal the rectum in its close fractures, which are not much significant, especially for one who has some study or experience
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many circumstances the way of restoring and straightening oneself: understand therefore.
If the vein is broken when the gut is ruptured, grasp the gut and insert the vein into it. Then place over it an outer dressing of cloth, and bind it, and let it remain for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills it.
As for the restoration of a fractured bone when there is an wound.
It is suitable for him upon whom such an accident has happened, especially if it is great, such as the thigh bone, or the collarbone, or the like of them, to immediately perform a bloodletting: if you testify to the condition of the bloodletting, such as we have said before
If blood flows from the wound, then it is necessary to perform an excision of it, that is to say to cut off the surrounding tissue: if there is nothing else present besides this
Then commence to restore the fracture on that very day: and do not delay it, if it does not happen spontaneously.
If indeed spontaneous union occurs, then defer the restoration until the ninth day, until the spontaneous union has taken place And do not approach it on the third or fourth day, for you will make him suffer other accidents
If however the fractured bone is prominent above the skin detected: then it is necessary to reduce it and bring it level with your hands with ease and slight extension
If it is not prepared for extension, and its equalization with your hands: then reduce it with this instrument And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is equal to the quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the quantity of the wound And therefore it is necessary for the physician to take from the patient three or four according to the quantity required for the cure of every kind of fracture.
And let it be rounded: neither too great nor too small, so that it does not be doubled upon compression in the hour of operation: and let the acute extremities be such as to have a margin in their extremities themselves And let the upper be to the greatness of the middle even unto the lower parts.
This is its form.
It is necessary in the operation that the acute extremity be bent over the protruding part of the bone: and that it be pressed with oil until the bone returns and is equal to the square of the equality
To strive therefore to make the extremities of the fracture equal to each other from below.
And if the extremities are slightly fractured and do not grasp the good instrument, then it is possible that one must cut off the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it
And if indeed it is not possible to reduce them as we have narrated throughout, then cut it off with the scissors which we have mentioned: or bind it with one of the bands in any way possible to him
And then remain that part of the bone which remains rough with small asperities and cortices.
When therefore the bone has been reduced, and when it has been found to have sustained an injury after reduction, you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this thing. For you confer great pain upon the patient when you complete the restoration of the limb as you do the bone
Then immerse the sponge in strong vinegar, black. And if in summer. And do not put upon the wound a greasy ointment, nor anything containing oil: lest it cause the wound to become infected and corrupted.
Then apply sponges at the end of your treatment to restore the bone. And remove the wound: thus, you will cut with scissors a hole in the swelling according to the extent of the wound
And take heed, do not tighten the wound with the fracture. Many have done this carelessly, and have caused great harm to their patients: either death, or corruption, or decay, that is, harm And let your treatment be gentle, lenient, diverse in the strictures of the remaining fractures
If the wound is bad, or is large, and you fear some harmful event which we have mentioned, and you find pain in the suspended part: then do not put sponges upon it And make incisions from strong leather patches in the place of the sponges, and tighten it with the leather
When however a day or two has passed, and the wound has begun to secrete pus: then remove the sponge which you placed upon it with the vinegar. Then apply unguents and ointments which are customary with us to heal the wound: such as the ointment of tetrapharmacon, and the like of it.
And it is expedient to tie off the ligament. And consider the wound every day in the morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed
It is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus may flow downwards easily.
If the wound days pass without consolidation and the pus is not evacuated, you know that there are small bone fragments there Therefore you must inquire about the wound with care How many of these bone fragments are separated from each other? Remove them and extract them. And what is not separated from them, and which penetrate the limb and cause pain, must be cut out, and removed in every way possible.
If there be swelling or infection of the wound, that is, inflammation, or other corruption and decomposition: then it is necessary that you oppose each one of these things with that which is suitable for its treatment, as has been mentioned in its chapter
And concerning that from which I have spoken, you must have a carefull mind when you break a bone, and it projects over the limb, as for example the thigh bone, and the patellas, and similar bones of the great limbs, that you do not presume its traction, for much harm often comes from that, indeed it is often better to let it go until it has decomposed
Perhaps indeed it will break by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then indeed care for the wound, if you see it: and otherwise let it go

Thus, take from these some or all of them: mix them with wine styptic, or with albumin or with vinegar: and apply them upon the knot in a lump, and bind them over the same good tightening.
And remove the tightening, neither keep it for many days. Then loosen the knot, and do it again, until the knot is released
Or bind over it a layer of lead foil which has been split in the middle. Lead has the property of loosening all that it touches of the members.

Concerning the care when restoring, and when the limb remains delicate after that, do not proceed contrary to its natural constitution.
When restoring a fractured bone, and the limb remains delicate afterwards, weak: then it is not done except for good causes For there are many solutions to the loosening of the ligament and its tightening: either because of the excessive tightening of the ligaments, until it hinders the nourishment of the limb: or because of the excessive amount of superfluous dressing which is not in its proper time: or because of the scarcity of blood in the body of the patient, and the weakening of the same.
And its treatment is to feed the patient and keep his body firm until the blood multiplies in it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb: so that it attracts plenty of nourishment to it And diligently anoint it with lukewarm water, until nourishment runs, and it returns to its natural shape.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored to their proper position
When the limb has been restored after healing from its fracture, and when the fractured bone is prominent or displaced: then it is necessary to massage the limb in such a way that the limb does not impede its natural action. Therefore it is not necessary to break the limb again for that purpose
And there are many proud doctors and restorers in our land who have done this.
And this operation is dangerous and harmful, leading to death, or great fear, as it is mortal.
But if the fracture and displacement are recent: then it is necessary to anoint it with lukewarm water containing soothing herbs such as elder leaves and the root of chamomile, and honey And make for it a plaster with a soothing plaster, such as gypsum made into a centre
Or take the slime of the elder root, and mix it with chicken fat, or with beeswax: and plaster it on with that
Or take pitch pine resin, and mix it with columbine flour, and other things that physicians call for diminishing consolidation
When the knot is loosened with persistent, ancient, and gentle friction, like that done with moist hands. The limb is moved in all parts at all times.</start_of_turn>
If, however, the swelling has preceded the pain, and has become strong, and has been converted into a stone, and has been driven out, necessity demands that it should be treated with iron: then it is necessary to cut above the swelling, to dissolve the continuation of the bone, and to remove what is superfluous from the knot or bone with fine scissors: and to apply ease in that place with care and attention.</start_of_turn>
Regarding the dislocation.</start_of_turn
Dislocation is the displacement of any articulation from its place.</start_of_turn Because it hinders its movement and deforms the limb.</start_of_turn And causes the emergence of excessive pain and wounds that are harmful.</start_of_turn
When, therefore, such a dislocation has occurred, it is necessary to expedite its restoration: neither should it be delayed at all.</start_of_turn
For if it is delayed, the place is left paralyzed, and perhaps with this the reduction of the dislocation is lost.</start_of_turn
Therefore it is not permitted to move or extend it in the hour following its displacement.</start_of_turn Because it causes many spasms and painful pains.</start_of_turn
But when it has occurred, it is necessary to order a bloodletting of the patient.</start_of_turn Then it is dismissed until the swelling subsides.
Then the limb is bound with warm water and oil Then it is reduced with ease
And all the limb is cared for so that its memory returns to its place.
I command that the head of the dislocation be set, even if it has happened in a fracture, from the upper part of the body down to the lower part of it
Concerning the care of the dislocation of the lower jaw
The two mandibles rarely dislocate except rarely
And the dislocation of both is done according to one of two methods. Or else they are removed from their places with a small movement, and they are softened lightly Or they are dislocated completely, without reduction, until they are softened to the breast, until the wound has healed, and it cannot hold together its own fragments, and it cannot close its own opening, and the tongue is involved with the speech
When the dislocation is small, then it returns by itself in various positions with little effort
If however the dislocation is complete, whole: then it must be immediately reduced, and it must not be delayed: and it is necessary that the head of the sufferer should remain still: and the doctor should insert his thumb of his hand into the root of the dislocation of the mandible between the bones of the dislocation, if the dislocation has happened from one side Or he should insert two fingers of his hand together, if the dislocation has happened from two sides And the rest of the fingers of his hand are outside, where they are equal.
And command the patient to loosen his teeth and bite down on all parts The physician must equal the jaw and push it, until it returns to its place
If it is difficult to reduce it, especially if both jaws are together: then give them a wet evaporation with warm water, and oil, until it is easy to reduce both of them: do not reduce them both at once, as we said
When they have returned and are equal, and the patient's jaw is closed, do not massage them: then place over their teeth little plugs of lead, with beeswax that has been made from wax: and oil of roses.
And it is bound with ease with soft bands.
And the patient should sleep on his back: and his head should be placed between two cushions, so that it does not move to the right or left: and do not force him, or make him chew anything: rather give him soft food to swallow, until when he moves his pain has subsided, and the jaw has returned to its place: then he may eat what he wants, and that should be given to him with ease
Do not work over the operation of his mouth during eating and drinking, and biting, until the jaw has settled and healed.
If it is difficult to reduce both jaws when they are displaced at some time, and they do not return to their places: then many times an accident of fever and bitter soda occurs: and perhaps it will be solved by the patient's vomiting, or perhaps by vomiting pure bile
When you have done this: then you know that it is lost, and many people die from this within ten days.
On the reduction of dislocations of the Furcula and the extremities of the humerus.
The Furcula does not dislocate from the inside outwards, because of its continuation with the sternum. It dislocates sometimes outwards: and this is declared by the sense.
And its restoration is that the patient should lie upon his back and extend his arms Then cover the place with your palm, for it will itself return
Then put upon it a pad and some wool, and bind it firmly
The extremities of the humerus which follow the elbow, and continue with it, do not dislocate, except rarely If they dislocate on some other day: then it is necessary to reduce it and restore it according to what has been said, and to prepare the limb for it.
Then put upon it a pad and some wool, and bind it firmly And command the patient to adhere to tranquility and quiet, until he is healed
And when the limb of the humerus has been thus reduced, when it is separated from its place also
Chapter XXVI
Know that the humerus does not dislocate except in three ways.
One of them is that it dislocates to the lower part of the elbow The second is that it dislocates to the part of the sternum. And perhaps it will be displaced to the upper part of the Humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it does not move to the back, because of the swelling And it does not move to the front, because of the nerves.
And most frequently it is displaced and comes down to the lower part towards the armpit: especially in those where the flesh is little Since it comes out quickly and enters quickly
In those where the flesh is much, it is the opposite, that is to say because it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty
And perhaps it happens to some people by blow or falling, and the Humerus is displaced by a blow of heat, and it is thought because of this that it is displaced
It is necessary therefore to examine it until you are certain of its displacement, and then you can proceed with its treatment.
It is recognized as displaced when it comes down towards the armpit: and you must distinguish between a displaced Humerus and a healthy Humerus For you will find among them a manifest difference, and you will find in the head of the Humerus a concavity and under the armpit at the touch of the hand as though it were a bone: and it is not possible for the infirm to reduce the hand to its proper position, nor to move it in any way.
Similarly, if it is displaced towards the chest or upwards: then you will find a clear mark, and it is not hidden
And in these displacements there is a simple reduction when it is recent, or when the infirm person is young.
And its reduction is such that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts. Then place your fingers under the edge of his neck, and elevate the wound with a steady hand to its proper place: and the minister raises his hand and extends it upwards. Then place the lower part downwards, and he himself will return quickly.
If, however, he does not return by that which we have said, and has been displaced from the beginning for several days: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and that an embolus be administered which will soften and soothe, like the decoction of rue, and barley, and meadowsweet in water, and that these be administered
Then the patient is placed lying on his back: and under his neck a sphere and a bandage are placed, which are equal in their degree of softness and tension.
Then the physician places his heel on the sphere, and raises the arm with force, and pulls the patient's hand downwards
And if necessary, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you make the strong hand which is longer than the patient to stand on the side, and to pass under the arm of the patient and bring the arm under the patient's neck: and elevate the patient's neck upwards until he is suspended in the air. And another minister pulls the patient's hand downwards through his belly If, however, the patient is slight: then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else, so that it is supported: for the displacement returns immediately.
And it is also reduced in another way: as when a long stick is planted in the ground, its head being rounded off like a cloak of a murderer: which is neither thick nor thin. Then it is placed under the lower part after the upper part of the stick has been covered with soft cloth: and the lower part remains fixed according to the length of the stick
Then the hand is extended downwards from the other side, and the body is extended from the other side with force: for the suture returns to its place with speed.
If its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then take care of it thus: take a stick whose length is equal to the distance of two outstretched arms, and whose width is equal to the distance of four fingers, and whose thickness is equal to the distance of two fingers: and let its head be rounded off, so that its entrance into the depth of the wound will be eased, according to this shape
Then bind over the rounded head soft cloth, so that it does not injure the wounded limb.
Then place under the wound the lower part of the limb, so that the hand or arm is extended over the stick down to the lower part: and bind the stick over the support and the arm and the hand.
Then place the arm over the step of the ladder sideways, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body be suspended from the other side. The suture enters immediately.
When it is thus complete the entrance has been made according to whatever way is possible: then it is necessary to place under the wound a sphere equal in quantity to the wool Then put on a prepared plaster of pounded barley with oil, and albumin on the whole arm, as is usual on the surface
Then string under the rib stricture descends: and the ligament is reversed above the plaster downwards: and its hand is connected to its column, and it is let go, neither hand moves for seven days.
It is necessary that food be given to the patient, until the limb is comfortable: for it is quicker for its healing.
Then, after seven days, or five, it is released when movement is restored. If, however, it remains firm and is not softened, then it is healed.
If, however, the suture is loosened many times, because of humidity which has happened to it, or because of some other cause: then it is necessary that there be applied to it cautery with three holes, according as is prescribed in the chapter on cautery
If this is done entirely, and the ligament is dissolved after seven days, and the suture is not confirmed, and the plaster and stricture above it are reduced somewhat, and it does not harden, and falls away, and does not rise up again: then you know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus are either cut, or extended, or softened: and you know that the suture will never take hold in its place.
As for dislocations which happen at the breast and the armpit, or at the back, they are reduced with a push and an extension with the hands: until they return, and the rest of the stricture and care are administered until healing takes place
If after healing there is still greatness in the limb, and tardiness in its movement: then the patient is bathed many times, until the greatness is softened, and it returns to its first nature.
(Text continues with a section on the treatment of dislocations of the elbow.)
Know that the dislocation of the cubital bone is difficult, and returns with difficulty: and it dislocates itself to all parts, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden: because it hangs under the skin and under the touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated And when you join the dislocated cubitum with the healthy one, it is declared to be conspicuous The joining is therefore necessary, and it cannot double the arm, nor touch it with the other arm.
And it is necessary to take care of it promptly, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before swelling sets in
For if swelling should set in, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is at the back For all kinds of dislocations are dangerous, and cause great pain: and they often descend with death.
And the restoration of it when it is possible to reduce it, is that you extend your hand with two hands: and the arm is expanded, and the hands of the physician are above the elbow and below it, and he urges the joining with the thumbs of both hands, or with the root of the palm of his hand, until it returns to its place
Or else the dislocation is to the front: then it returns so that you can double your hand with your hand, until it touches with the root of the palm of your hand the dislocated cubitum.
If it does not obey the dislocation for reduction, then you must administer prolonged extension, strongly: and it is that two assistants extend the arm: and they hold it firmly even, so that it does not move during the extension
Then the arm is returned to its entirety after it has been included over the hand with a doubled long panus or a wide ligament.
And when the physician grasps the hand with his grasp, he should anoint it with oil: so that it may be from the things that aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease Then he drives the grasp forward with an urging movement until it returns
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place over it a dressing in which there is styptic material, and drying with alum: and it should be bound securely. And the arm is suspended from the neck of the patient: and it is allowed to rest for a few days.
Then it is healed If, however, the grasp remains fixed in its place, then the ligament is loosened from it and it is allowed to rest.
And if the grasp is not properly bound, then repeat the dressing and the ligament and allow it to rest for a few days until it is bound securely. Then it is healed.
If, however, there is a degree of severity after the reduction of the grasp, and the position is in motion: then administer moistness in the bath, and gentle friction, and compression, until the swelling subsides, or place over the grasp a compress, that is, the wool of a sheep's fleece Then it is bound, and it is allowed to rest day and night Then take away the bandage, and put it into a bath When it is wet, then gently massage the grasp with temperate friction Then again, repeat over the belly of the patient once, twice, and thrice with the bath inside, until it is softened.
And if desired, place upon the belly of the patient a soft cushion of wool, moistened with butter, and bind over it.</start_of_turn>
Do this often.</start_of_turn> The body itself will soften, and will return to its normal state.</start_of_turn>

The limb of Mahassem is often dislocated, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.</start_of_turn
And its reduction is easy.</start_of_turn Unlike other joints.</start_of_turn Indeed, it is necessary that speed be in the reduction of its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before it has cooled down.</start_of_turn
And the reduction of the dislocation is like this: place the lower arm of Mahassem upon a board, and extend the hand: and the doctor places his palm upon the prominence of the joint and urges it until it returns.</start_of_turn
Indeed, it is necessary to consider, if the palm has receded towards the elbow: then the patient places the back of his hand on the board at the extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is proceeding towards the elbow, then the position of the hand is inside the board, that is, the inner part of the hand is placed upon the board, so that the hand of the doctor rests upon the prominence of the joint.</start_of_turn
If he returns immediately, it is good; but if not, then bind him with packing around the swelling while it is still soft: and let it go, and do not touch it again.
If he does not tolerate it, neither will a forced reduction be of any avail after a few days: for truly the ligature will remain as before according to the hardness of the swelling. And it does no harm to the patient anything, except that the hand is softened, and nothing can be bound. Then you know that the nerves are severed, or torn away.
There is no skill in this, except to bind it with cautery near the base of the swelling. And perhaps it will do no harm at all.
When the soul does not return, then place over it the packing which we mentioned. And then bind it and let it go for five days: afterwards dissolve it and let it go, and exercise the hand.
If however its motion is hindered, and something heavy has occurred in it: then soften it with warm water and gentle friction, until it is softened

When the Digit is dislocated from its place to any part, or from the palm of the hand to the dorsum, or from the bones of the hand: then extend the Digit and drive back the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns
And then support the head of the Digit, and suspend it over the part from which it is dislocated, and let it go in two days.
And then suspend it, and extend it, until it is perfectly straight on that day.
When the night is advanced, bind the limb according to the account given: and do not cease to resolve it daily: and exercise it with movement: and bind it in the night.
Repeat this daily until it is healed.
Do the same with the hand if it is dislocated towards the palm: bind it to the same part and perform the operation as before until it heals.
Do the same in the case of dislocation towards the other parts
Concerning the cure of dislocation of the shoulder blade.
When anyone has suffered dislocation of the shoulder blade, or complete dislocation of the collarbone, or has displaced many vertebrae from their places: then it is not necessary in such a case to perform any cure, because the moisture is enough for the sick person
And the sign of this is that the patient is driven out without his will, and cannot keep his limbs: and they soften many parts of his body, or two arms or two legs, or one of his members: or displaces one vertebra from its place
For many cause many displacements, because many are displaced towards four parts.
The displacement towards the back is called Gibbositas
The cure in this case is, if the Gibbositas has happened from infancy: then no cure is necessary, neither is there any complete recovery from it
But in these cases, which happen from blow or similar things, as the ancients have said in a long discourse about the methods of treatment Most of them do not return when they are perfected
I have indeed abbreviated it because its explanation is not appropriate in relation to my proximity to the matter: and the exposition which I have given of it, and the formation of its instrument in a different manner from that which they have shown, and the exposition of itself.
Therefore I say, that in the swelling which arises from the front part, there is neither wit nor healing to be found
Similarly in that which happens on both sides also
And it is not cured except for that which happens in its own back, as I have said: and that is, that the patient should extend himself over his face over a level platform near the wall: and let the moisture expand under that, so that the chest is not injured
Then put a fixed stool of wood in the fire in the ground, at the foot of the bed, round about the head: and another stool of wood round about the feet at the other foot of the bed: and let the attendant hold the stool, and let it not be fixed in the fire. And let the other attendant hold the other.
Then put on the head of the patient, and under his chin a soft ligament, firm: and stretch out the extremity of the ligament to the wood which is at his head: and bind it in that place
Then string another ligament above his shoulders and above his knees and at his buttocks. And then gather up all the ligaments and bind them in the other wood which is at his feet
And let all the attendants extend themselves to the part of the limb of the ligaments, and do not move the two woods from their places where they are fixed: indeed they are not fixed, as we said.
And the physician lays his hands upon the Spondilem with force, until it returns: or lays them upon a board. And then he fixes his feet upon the board, until it returns.
If it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms long.</start_of_turn> And hold it in the wall where we said it is close to the patient, in the place where the extremity of the board enters.</start_of_turn> And then place the middle of the board upon the Spondilem.</start_of_turn> And the physician lays his feet upon the other extremity.</start_of_turn And bind it well, until the Spondilem is firm, and returns to its place.</start_of_turn>
And if you wish, work with the ankle which is included with your hand: and it is that you put two boards, each as long as an arm, under the foot at the back of the patient, and under the head of it two boards.</start_of_turn
And let the length which is between all the boards be one palm: and it is finished in every place where there is a hole through which the ankle turns: and fix all the boards firmly to the ground, so that nothing moves.</start_of_turn
And put in a round board: and it is the ankle which revolves in two holes of the two boards.</start_of_turn
And at its extremity is a hole in which the bone is fixed, which is as long as a palm with that which revolves: and in two other boards it is like that.</start_of_turn
And string the ligaments which you bound in the chest of the patient around the ankle which is at his head: and the ligaments which you bound in the thighs of the patient around the ankles which are at his feet.</start_of_turn
Then the minister shall remain at every turn, who shall twist his hand with the linen, with which the turn is reversed: and the physician shall equal the Spondilem, as we have said.
This is the form of the turn which follows.
Then when Spondilis returns and the place is equal, then it is necessary to place the soaked sponges with albumin or oil Then the sponge. Then place above the sponge a star of table, whose width is three fingers, or somewhat less And its length is the quantity which will cover the place of Gibbositatis, and over the sound part of Spondilis
Then it is bound with the ligation which we have said And the patient is kept nourished with delicate food until he is healed
And if anything remains prominent in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines which soften and soothe, along with the administration of the tablet which we mentioned in the long term And they are administered on that lamina of lead.
And when it happens that there is prominence at the last Spondilium of the back: and it is thought to be dislocation, and that is the bone which is now prominent Therefore it is not necessary to assume that for this reason: for perhaps it will happen that death will happen from this medicine.
 On the treatment of a dislocated Anchae.
Know that the ligatures of the Anchae and of the humerus do not happen except for dislocation: and that this does not happen except for a slight displacement and a concavity.
The suture is loosened from the Anchae according to four parts. The suture is loosened from the Anchae according to the front and back parts
The suture is loosened from the Anchae mostly towards the inside: slightly towards the front and slightly towards the back.
The treatment of its dislocation towards the inside is as follows: when you join the limb of the healthy person with the limb of the patient, the latter is longer: and the knee of the patient is more prominent than that of the healthy person, and the patient cannot double up his foot at the ankle: and this is the place which follows the heel after the heel has passed over the knee, because the head of the thigh bone has already gone there.
The sign of its dislocation towards the outside is that these accidents are contrary to these.
The sign of its dislocation towards the front is that it expands the healthy limb according to the complement: indeed it does not double itself without pain at the knee: and if it tries to walk, it cannot do so without going over the front: and it retains its urine, and its heel is elevated, and there is swelling at walking.
The sign of its dislocation towards the back is that it does not expand the knee, and it cannot double itself before it doubles the ankle: and the leg is also shorter than the other: and the ankle is soft, and the head of the thigh bone is at the level of the hip bone
The reduction of this dislocation is indeed of the kind which involves bringing together what has been separated for a long time, which has become old with the possessor of the same thing. Then if you perform the reduction and it remains according to its position: then there is no remaining cure in this matter It is not right that an assumption should be made of him.
However, in the case of dislocation whose displacement is slight, and which belongs to the class of four dislocations, then proceed as follows: grasp firmly and extend the dislocation inwards and outwards, and move the displaced limb from side to side: often the dislocation will return by itself and no further treatment will be necessary.
If it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant, and to extend the limb from its lower part: either with the hands alone or with a ligature which is bound above the knee: and another assistant should extend the hand above, so that the hand is introduced under the toes. Then bind with a soft ligature over the root of the thigh, and hold the extremity of the other ligament.
The extension should be from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the pelvis, or from the posterior part to the part of the back.
And let the extension be uniform throughout, until the patient is lifted up with his body from the ground, and remains hanging down
And this is a common kind of extension among the four kinds.
If the dislocation returns in this way, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary in every case to use the proper treatment.
In reducing it, however, when the dislocation is inwards, it is necessary to support the sound limb over its lateral surface. Then bind the ligament over the root of the thigh, at the place between the head of the thigh and the groin And extend the ligament upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the pelvis
Then another strong arm shall take hold of the arm, and shall diminish the great place of the lower coxae, and shall extend outwards to the outer limit. And then it returns to its place.
This is the easier kind of treatment for this limb.
If, however, he excuses himself from thee, and does not obey thy order with this kind of treatment: then it is necessary that two strong legs be bound along with the strong ligament, over the two ankles, and over the two knees: and let the length of each be as much as four fingers; and let the affected limb be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the limb is suspended above the head of the bone which is in the middle: and let its length from the ground be as much as the quantity of two arms
Then command a strong youth to grasp the head of the coxae: and suspend it with the limb together with another: and let another strong youth push forward, grasping the coxae with force, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In its return, however, when there is displacement outwards, it is necessary that the limb lie upon a board, as we have said in the case of a gibbosity. And bind the ligament over the thigh itself, and over the breastbone
Then two boards are placed, one at the foot and the other at the head of the limb. Then a board is placed added in the middle of the foot, fixed well: over which are placed wet cloths, so that it does not hurt the limb: and let the board be between the thighs, so that it does not draw towards the lower parts at the extension.
Then let every minister extend to his own part, and the doctor will equal the reduction of his hand.
If anyone obeys towards reversion, it will be good.
And if not, place above itself a board, and press it down, as we said in the swelling, equally. It is true that the patient should lie upon his sound side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then it is necessary that the limb of the patient be extended quickly: and it is in accordance with this narration that the doctor places his right palm of his hand upon the groin of the patient Then tighten it with the other limb, and with that tightening place the limb extended downwards to the part of the knee
And if the dislocation is backward: then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, but rather elevated above the earth: indeed it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard object, as it is necessary that it be when it is displaced widely outwards, as we said concerning its lying upon two cushions: and it is upon its face, and the ligaments extended: as we said before
And it is necessary that pressure be administered with a board also over the place from which the joint has gone out of place, when the reduction is completed as it is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden to you: and it is that you extend both limbs of the patient: and when you take away these equal, and the patient tightens his limb and expands it without groaning: then you know that the limb has returned, as it is necessary: then join together the two thighs, and place a bandage, and tighten with the thumb: so that it does not move outwards to any part: and the patient should rest quietly for three days, or four.
Then restore the ligament, and the patch, and compare the leg with the other: when you see that they are equal in position, then you know that the dislocation is fixed: therefore the patient is able to walk.
Or if you wish to soften it: then retract it, and apply the patch again, and bind it according to your first bandage: and let it go for three days also.
Then restore it and make it slow in its walking over both sides for a few days, until it is comfortable again.
Concerning dislocations of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways.</start_of_turn> It is displaced outwards, inwards, or downwards, that is, backwards.</start_of_turn
It is not displaced forwards.</start_of_turn
Its sign is that the patient applies his leg to his hip.</start_of_turn But if it does not adhere to the hip: then you know that the knee is dislocated.</start_of_turn
And the restoration of all these kinds of dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he can extend his leg over it, and that you place his heel between his feet.</start_of_turn
Then bind the knee to his palms, and make it straight between your fingers over the knee.</start_of_turn Then apply with your palm two sides of the knee with force.</start_of_turn And another assistant extends his foot, until the knee returns to its place.</start_of_turn
And a sign of recovery is that the thigh should adhere to the hip in ease, not laboriously.
Then apply the poultice, and let the thigh adhere to the hip Then bind it together with three or four days, or so. Then make it whole
And let him not walk too much for a few days, until he has recovered
If, however, the thigh remains stiff after the reduction, then administer extension with ligatures, as was mentioned in the treatment of the knee, until it returns
(Continuation of previous translation
The calcaneus sometimes returns spontaneously, and sometimes it is dislocated according to its complement And its dislocation is either inward or outward
And the sign of its dislocation is that the heel is swollen, proceeding to the part towards which it is dislocated
The treatment of its removal is easy: it is to extend it easily with the hands, and to make it equal, until it returns.
But when it is dislocated according to its complement, it is necessary to make the patient sit upright: and hold it firmly with a strong hand from behind the back of his heel, in the middle of it.
Then take hold of it with your right hand from the upper part of his foot, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the level of the ankle
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand Then when the left hand has two fingers.
Then spread out the left hand, and push the heel of the foot with the right hand towards the knee without straining.
Do it twice like we have said.
Then push the heel of the foot towards the knee in the third space, and draw the nail backwards
If therefore it returns to one space, or to two in this way, and you have equated the foot, it is good: and if not, do the operation again above it: for it will return itself
If however it is prohibited to reduce it as we have said: then make the patient lie upon his back on the earth, and fix the heel in the earth firmly, so that it rests between the patient's thighs: and you will have involved the thumb over the wound so that it does not damage the patient.
Then take hold of the patient's heel: afterwards extend the other minister of the foot, either with the hands or with a ligament which is bound over the ankle Then extend all the ministers against the extension of the companion: and the heel will be stable between the two thighs of the patient holding it, so that it does not pull the body downwards towards the extension.
Then the doctor will equal the dislocation with his hands, and the other minister will hold the healthy knee towards the lower parts For the dislocation returns with speed.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and it is declared that the reduction is complete: then apply a compress and bandage, and bind up the wound firmly with ligaments, and bind the foot to the ankle
And it is necessary to beware of nerves, which are above the heel behind, so that there is no ligament above them which is taut and damages them
Then discharge him in two days, or three. If the ligament is softened, bind it up: then discharge him on the third or fourth day. Prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. If he attempts to walk before that space has healed, there is no security that the dislocation will be dissolved, and it will be corrupted, and will not recover afterwards
If there should be a soft swelling: then it is necessary that you apply in its sedation the thing about which we spoke elsewhere in connection with treatment, and embrocation, until it is removed
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that it be equated that which is dislocated from its place with little extension without laborious work: and this is not difficult, indeed it is easy.
If however the dislocation is in some knot of the ankle of the foot: then it is necessary that the patient sit down and rest his foot on the ground, or on a board: and he should be as if he were walking
Then stand up, and place your foot on that which is opposite to those joints. Then clench your foot with your foot as if with a vice, until it returns, and you will see that it is equal and does not appear in prominence
Then place under the middle of his foot a board which can grasp the whole foot, which has two heads Then bind it up tightly for three days. Then discharge him, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is bound up and secured from reduction
On the kinds of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When anything of this kind happens, and you wish to cure it and restore it, many things follow: many of them are harmful to the cure itself. Therefore, it is not permitted to take over the treatment without being skilled in the art of long-term treatment, merciful, pious, prompt, courageous, and not arrogant: and to be subservient to the advice of physicians who sit at the foot of the bed only for warmth. And let him be dismissed from the sickbed with the judge's leave. Except that you think, or hope, that the cure will be found in the softening of the pain, and in what appears to be something hopeful. Try to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the case, before the swelling becomes cold
If the limb returns, as we wish, then administer a regimen that will settle the swelling: and care for the wound with what is suitable for it from desiccant unguents.
If the dislocation is with a fracture, and they are separated from each other in the bone, then try to remove them, and do what we said concerning simple pains in those places
And be careful according to your virtue, and protect yourself from the entrance of fear: according to what has been said before to you
For it is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more beneficial for your body in your time.
